  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

  <span id="freeText15644523550929974773" style="display:none">Despite constant efforts to declutter your home, do papers still accumulate like snowdrifts and clothes pile up like a tangled mess of noodles?<p>Japanese cleaning consultant Marie Kondo takes tidying to a whole new level, promising that if you properly simplify and organize your home once, you'll never have to do it again. Most methods advocate a room-by-room or little-by-little approach, which doom you to pick away at your piles of stuff forever. The KonMari Method, with its revolutionary category-by-category system, leads to lasting results. In fact, none of Kondo's clients have lapsed (and she still has a three-month waiting list).</p><p>With detailed guidance for determining which items in your house "spark joy" (and which don't), this international best seller featuring Tokyo's newest lifestyle phenomenon will help you clear your clutter and enjoy the unique magic of a tidy home - and the calm, motivated mindset it can inspire.</p></span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/361753-get-rid-of-anything-that-doesn-t-spark">&quot;Get rid of anything that doesn&#39;t spark joy.&quot; That&#39;s a cute idea, but there&#39;s chemotherapy medicine in the medicine cabinet, and it requires soul-searching to find the joy in that. I kept thinking, &quot;Surely she&#39;ll address the hard stuff soon?!&quot; I haven&#39;t found that part yet.</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/891126-melissa"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1420067482i/891126._UX100_CR0,8,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/891126-melissa">Melissa</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The author did address that, in a way. It is not the medicine that sparks joy, but the hope of beating cancer. And truly, if you don't hope of beating<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">‚Ä¶more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The author did address that, in a way. It is not the medicine that sparks joy, but the hope of beating cancer. And truly, if you don't hope of beating cancer, perhaps you should throw away the medicine and live the life you have now! My mother-in-law did that after about a year of cancer treatment. The last few months of her life were beautiful, and truly did bring her joy. <br /><br />Only you can decide what will bring you joy. Follow your instinct!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/276057-is-she-saying-to-get-all-of-your-clothes">Is she saying to get all of your clothes out onto the floor, and then sort them by category, and then purge by category? I feel like it would be difficult to sort by category if you&#39;ve just dumped every category of clothing into one big pile.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_276057'><a id="like_count_community_question_276057" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/276057?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">34 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F22318578-the-life-changing-magic-of-tidying-up">like</a></span>

<a href="/questions/276057-is-she-saying-to-get-all-of-your-clothes">See all 15 answers</a>
</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/1545100-jennifer-sardam"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1488406065i/1545100._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/1545100-jennifer-sardam">Jennifer Sardam</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No, in my experience so far, the book seems to encourage the reader and participant in this minimalist project to go by his or her own guidance system<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">‚Ä¶more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No, in my experience so far, the book seems to encourage the reader and participant in this minimalist project to go by his or her own guidance system and choose which things to keep based on the feelings he or she gets from each object. This is the first time I've seen advice like this in a book on decluttering, and I LOVE it. Unique and inspiring. This makes sense to me, because -- at least in my own experience -- we often fail to be able to get rid of things, because there is some emotion or mental construct connected with an item ... whether it's the thought that "I might read this book one day," or "It reminds me of my childhood."<br /><br />For example, she advises to first gather all of the clothing from everywhere in the house -- not just that which is already in the closet or in the drawers of the bedroom dresser ... but EVERY single scrap that's wandering about the resident's home (In fact, she mentions that, with her clients, when they do this exercise together with her, if they forget to bring in an item from another room ... she does not even give them an alibi. The rule is that the item has to go!)<br /><br />Although it sounds like a gargantuan process, she says you must physically take each garment or accessory in hand and go by the way it makes you feel. If you are not enlivened somehow by it, then it has to go. According to the author, her principles followed closely will result in the participant/reader never backsliding to having to repeat the process all over again. It is supposedly foolproof and maintainable for life. <br /><br />I am only halfway through the book, but I'm intrigued and hopeful, as I've already gotten into the spirit of it, and I managed to rid myself of two HUGE contractor bags full of Tupperware and kitchen drawer odds and ends, things like unnecessary plastic cups with theme park designs, old chipped plates, etc. (I did all of that BEFORE I realized I was supposed to FIRST begin the entire process with the closet and then move in sequence from there ... whoops!)<br /><br />I have seen those reality TV shows, and I can tell you that I'm by NO means a hoarder; but I have lived most of my life suffering the frustration of ADHD and the heaviness of feeling/being "chronically disorganized," mostly with books, clothing and loads of "important paperwork" (spending hours upon hours some nights just trying to find that one critical paper for the next day ... ugh, so MUCH wasted energy and time). So this book is a breath of fresh air that I welcome with open arms!<br /><br />If you are considering buying or reading this book, and it helps, I will sum up the major point that I get from it so far; and that is that we must realize that not everything or everyone is meant to be in our lives for a finite amount of time. We cannot go through a lifetime collecting and keeping everything that "sticks" to us, is given to us, or that we accumulate in some way. Some gifts, no matter how well-meaning, don't fit who we are. Some people are just trying to unload their own tangible crap on us, and that's unfair of them (she goes into that, too -- don't just rid yourself of stuff by giving it to a poor, unsuspecting relative).<br /><br />The stuff that enters our lives has its defined purpose ... sometimes it's just a fleeting moment to be enjoyed. The greeting card has done its deed when it conveys the feeling of love and warmth across a thousand miles from a loved one; and yet many of us feel compelled to keep them all, carting them from one residence to another, piled in boxes as if the recipient might somehow be secretly notified and then hurt at the moment that we throw them away. If anything, that person would probably be sad that their cards caused so much work instead of joy for the recipient!<br /><br />Books are often meant to be read when the feeling strikes us to choose them and to immerse ourselves in reading their stories. Years later, their time to be read has passed, and it is okay to donate them to someone else, to move past that nagging feeling that we didn't finish something. <br /><br />The purpose of the book could have been to convey one simple truth to the owner. That cocktail dress you never really wore? Though you loved seeing it hanging in your closet, a deep satiny blue, beckoning you to the life you dreamed of ... but maybe never quite lived? It's okay to release it, get rid of the guilt of never wearing it. You were changed by it just the same, because possibly it taught you who you were not going to be in this life.<br /><br />Really, it's okay ... that's what I'm learning from this powerful book ... after 42 years of feeling bad about throwing things away and trying to find better organizing tips or more storage space. Those actions don't fix the problem. They only allow the problems to live on ... deal with your stuff! That's the answer she's giving us here. I think the message here IS truly life-changing as the title implies. Enjoy!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

<div id="review_1171393193" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1171393193" />
    <a title="Diane Yannick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1253673-diane-yannick"><img alt="Diane Yannick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468376907p2/1253673.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1171393193?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Yannick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Yannick" href="/user/show/1253673-diane-yannick">Diane Yannick</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1171393193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1580981517228876621">There is no rating low enough to compensate for the way this book has ruined my life. Whatever you do, don't read it as it will haunt you. This is a long review but it behooves you to stick with it. <br/><br/>To begin, you have to touch each and every possession and ask yourself if it brings you joy. If it doesn't, it needs to be discarded. Do you know how hard it is to summon joy for beige underwear or Neosporin? Yet summon you must. I like my carrot peeler but is joy too strong a word? <br/><br/>It gets tougher. </span>
  <span id="freeText1580981517228876621" style="display:none">There is no rating low enough to compensate for the way this book has ruined my life. Whatever you do, don't read it as it will haunt you. This is a long review but it behooves you to stick with it. <br /><br />To begin, you have to touch each and every possession and ask yourself if it brings you joy. If it doesn't, it needs to be discarded. Do you know how hard it is to summon joy for beige underwear or Neosporin? Yet summon you must. I like my carrot peeler but is joy too strong a word? <br /><br />It gets tougher. Not only must you "apply hands" to every piece of clothing while checking for your joy response, you must fold your clothes in very specific ways in order to find each piece's "sweet spot". I never knew that "Every piece of clothing has it's own 'sweet spot' where it feels just right". This will lead to an historical moment when "your mind and the piece of clothing connect." Don't forget to thank your clothes for protecting your body. Thank your accessories for making you beautiful. "Express your appreciation to every item that supported you during the day."<br /><br />Never, never "ball up your socks" as they can't get their much deserved rest that way. They need to breathe a sigh of relief. You must visit your off season clothes to "let them know you care and look forward to wearing them when they're next in season. This kind of communication helps your clothes stay vibrant and keeps your relationship with them alive longer."<br /><br />Everything must be stored standing up rather than laid flat. "Stacking weakens and exhausts the things that bear the weight of the pile. Just imagine how you would feel if you were forced to carry a heavy load for hours?"<br /><br />You must empty your hand bag every day."Being packed all the time, even when not in use, must feel something like going to bed with a full stomach." When you get a new phone, it is kind to text your old one with a message of thanks for its service.<br /><br />Every single thing must be in its assigned place before you go to bed." Have you ever thought about what it would be like to have no fixed address?" It is equally important for our possessions to have "that assurance that there is a place for them to return to."<br /><br />If we greet our house properly it will " be happy to tell me what the family no longer needs and where to put the things remaining so that the family can be comfortable and happy in this space." Not sure if you do this before or after applying hands and checking your joy response. It did come near the end of the book, if that helps.<br /><br />So what do the things that get discarded for not sparking joy feel? "I think they simply want to leave. Lying forgotten in the closet, they know better than anyone else that they are not bringing you joy." <br /><br />The people who tidy as this book recommend experience a change in their appearance. "Their figures are more streamlined, their skin is more radiant, and their eyes shine brighter."<br /><br />Unfortunately, my eyes are crossed, I've gained 10 pounds, and I feel hives ready to pop. I had no idea when I picked up this pretty little book that it was going to necessitate my search for a live-in psychiatrist. I had NO freaking idea that I was squashing the self-esteem of my possessions. I can NOT find those folding sweet spots and everything in my drawers is in a mosh pit. My hand bag looks sad every time I walk by its nightly fullness. My socks are falling down. I'm not sure why. I hate the word joy now. I'm sleep deprived. My possessions call out to me for help but I don't know if they want me to summon joy or if they want to leave. The other day my husband heard me thanking my underwear for staying up all day. I'm not sure how much longer I'll be married.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1580981517228876621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1093033059" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1093033059" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1093033059?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2014">finished-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1093033059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7126874727702909650">How do I get into messes like this? Reading books that have words like "tidying" in their title, I mean. Well, there's a story behind it.<br/><br/>The Good Wife and I are trying to purge, to clean up after decades of marriage, kids, three moves, etc. "We've got to do something about all this <i>stuff</i> we keep bumping into or dusting," I keep saying, "something more than the penny ante efforts we're doing so far."<br/><br/>Enter a <i>New York Times</i> feature on THIS book, calling it the greatest thing since sliced sushi:<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.nytimes.com/2014/10/23/garden/home-organization-advice-from-marie-kondo.html?_r=0'>http</a></span>
  <span id="freeText7126874727702909650" style="display:none">How do I get into messes like this? Reading books that have words like "tidying" in their title, I mean. Well, there's a story behind it.<br /><br />The Good Wife and I are trying to purge, to clean up after decades of marriage, kids, three moves, etc. "We've got to do something about all this <i>stuff</i> we keep bumping into or dusting," I keep saying, "something more than the penny ante efforts we're doing so far."<br /><br />Enter a <i>New York Times</i> feature on THIS book, calling it the greatest thing since sliced sushi:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2014/10/23/garden/home-organization-advice-from-marie-kondo.html?_r=0">http://www.nytimes.com/2014/10/23/gar...</a><br /><br />"That does it," I told my wife. "We're both reading this on the Kindle."<br /><br />"You first," she said.<br /><br />I should've known right there. It was kind of like the puppy we picked out 10 year ago. "He's a little mouthy," the guy at the shelter said. <br /><br />You'd think an English major would know foreshadowing when he heard it.<br /><br />Anyway, I'm reading this book and reading this book and saying to myself, "The <i>Times</i> article IS the book. Free, too! Why am I reading all this filler, this clutter, these words in need of TIDYING up?"<br /><br />That said, I should give the book a star back just for the punch line, <b>"Does it spark joy?"</b> It's what you say when holding some up-for-tossing item in your hand. "Does it spark joy?" If not, heave and ho, my friends.<br /><br />Just now, I looked around the kitchen with my wife at my side. "Nothing in here sparks joy...," I said.<br /><br />"<i>I'm</i> in the kitchen," she replied firmly.<br /><br />"No, no. Stuff, I mean."<br /><br />Anyway, that's the gist of the book. As for her system, Marie Kondo goes into houses (she does this for a living) and starts her ritual by singing its praises. Then she practices the praise of reduction. <br /><br />She's grateful in her own home, too. For instance, she brings her purse home from work, empties it, puts stuff in its place (fear not, it has a place), then thanks her purse for another job well done. In the morning it all goes back in. Says it extends the life of her purse. You tell me -- "tidying" or <i>tidying</i>?<br /><br />Marie also says to congregate everything by category in one spot. Start with clothes. Never room by room where clothes may be found, but ALL clothes from the WHOLE house in ONE spot on the floor (if you have a room big enough, and you don't). Now hold each piece of clothing, one by one. "Does this spark joy?" Bzzt.<br /><br />(And that's just the clothes... you still have books, papers, mementos, and personal stuff ahead and in that order.)<br /><br />The only other thing I recall is hanging stuff in the closet. Light clothes (color, material) left, darker and heavier right. That's after you've tossed 87.9% of it.<br /><br />Anyway, my wife is getting cute now. "Done," I said. "Now you."<br /><br />"Why don't you tell me the highlights instead," she said, already spooked by the "spark-any-joy?" talk.<br /><br />"Wha-?"<br /><br />Yep. Just like the former puppy that by now has consumed half the house because he's "a little mouthy." Should've seen it coming from the get-go -- the minute I got the "you first" line. <br /><br />Bottom line: You can't "tidy" a house in ruthless Marie Kondo fashion when you're 40% of a pair (the other 60% having a Security Council Veto, among other super powers). But that's marriage... a very untidy thing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7126874727702909650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1365594415" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1365594415" />
    <a title="Ariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6536286-ariel"><img alt="Ariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530557165p2/6536286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1365594415?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ariel" href="/user/show/6536286-ariel">Ariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1365594415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5481312925997219901">"You will never use spare buttons." WORDS WE ALL NEED TO HEAR.<br/><br/>I really thoroughly loved this book. I've always been interested in minimalism - trying to stop material goods being the things that bring us happiness, and not owning more than we need - and picked this book up after hearing a few people around me enjoy it. I was hoping it would give me a few tips on how to lead a more clutter-free existence, and while it accomplished that, it also did so much more. <br/><br/>The thing that most stuck me about</span>
  <span id="freeText5481312925997219901" style="display:none">"You will never use spare buttons." WORDS WE ALL NEED TO HEAR.<br /><br />I really thoroughly loved this book. I've always been interested in minimalism - trying to stop material goods being the things that bring us happiness, and not owning more than we need - and picked this book up after hearing a few people around me enjoy it. I was hoping it would give me a few tips on how to lead a more clutter-free existence, and while it accomplished that, it also did so much more. <br /><br />The thing that most stuck me about this book was the immense respect that the author, (who feels like my friend after reading this, a gentle companion through this process), shows to all possessions. She understands that this is an emotional process, and her ideas are not about guilt. She isn't saying "You're a fool for allowing all of this garbage into your life! Get rid of it all at once!" Instead she's saying "All of these things came into your life for a reason, take your time considering them, and then thank them and let them go onto the next stage of existence." It's all so kind and loving and made me not feel guilty or fear the process.<br /><br />I also really liked the structure of this book. It has a solid introduction, a clear process, a section of tips, and then a conclusion. It's very straightforward and doesn't feel complex or gimmicky. It all feels like common sense. It also had key points in bold which helped in figuring out the main thesis of different sections.<br /><br />The book focuses on tidying your house, but for me (and most of the people reading this) we don't own a house, we own one room. I don't think this matters for two reasons. Firstly, the ideas and methods in this book can be perfectly applied to your space, no matter how extensive it is. It's all about creating the most positive space imaginable, and that is very achievable with just one room. Secondly, this book was very much a philosophy. It's about learning to reflect on the relationship between you and your possessions and is something I will carry with me moving forward in life. Someday I will move out of my parents house and into my own house/apartment and I am extremely grateful that I have read this book and will be able to start that journey with this knowledge.<br /><br />Thank you, Marie, for sharing what you've learned, I'm very excited to start my journey of only surrounding myself with things that I love and bring me joy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5481312925997219901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2986363426653874134" style="display:none">"Personally, I recommend hanging sponges outside, such as on the veranda."<br /><br />Quite possibly the funniest line I have ever read in a book. Because she is dead serious.<br /><br />Although there were several helpful sections about how to declutter and organize your home, much of this book is ridiculously funny. <br /><br />This woman obviously has no children (and no life) as she speaks to all her inanimate objects daily, empties her purse EVERY SINGLE DAY, and doesn't own a dish rack, preferring instead to put all her dishes "on the veranda" to dry.               <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21901488-kirstie?shelf=2014_read">2014_read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1082453986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1510018752065006491">This book just spoke to me, for whatever reason, although I can see why others might not get much out of it. No matter what the subject is, I love reading books by people that are truly passionate about the subject matter, and one thing is for sure: Marie Kondo is passionate about tidying. <br/><br/>Despite all of that, this book isn't <i>really</i> about tidying, at its core. It's about living very intentionally and allowing your surroundings and possessions to become an expression of yourself. If that last sen</span>
  <span id="freeText1510018752065006491" style="display:none">This book just spoke to me, for whatever reason, although I can see why others might not get much out of it. No matter what the subject is, I love reading books by people that are truly passionate about the subject matter, and one thing is for sure: Marie Kondo is passionate about tidying. <br /><br />Despite all of that, this book isn't <i>really</i> about tidying, at its core. It's about living very intentionally and allowing your surroundings and possessions to become an expression of yourself. If that last sentence made you roll your eyes, skip this book, but if you like the idea of finding meaning in the things that you own, you'll enjoy this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1510018752065006491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2707601798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2707601798" />
    <a title="Miranda Reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads"><img alt="Miranda Reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592243041p2/71848701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2707601798?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miranda Reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miranda Reads" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads">Miranda Reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2707601798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9081042470650614423"><blockquote>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1584849321i/29144613._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='200' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</blockquote>Looking for a new book but don't want to commit? Check out my latest <b>
  <u>
    <a href='https://youtu.be/NjzZfDB4NKA' rel='nofollow noopener'>BooktTube Video: One & Done </a>
  </u>
</b> - all about fabulous standalones! <br/><br/>Now that you know this one made the list - check out the video to see the rest!<br/><br/><b>
  <u>The Written Review</u>
</b>:<blockquote> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1567366972i/28085229._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='400' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/><i>The question of what you want to own is actually the question of how you want to live your life.</i>
</blockquote>By no exaggeration, Marie Kond≈ç has spent <b>her entire life tidying.</b><br/><br/>She tidied when she was a child up until her adulthood - and over those decades, she'</span>
  <span id="freeText9081042470650614423" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1584849321i/29144613._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Looking for a new book but don't want to commit? Check out my latest <b>
  <u>
    <a href="https://youtu.be/NjzZfDB4NKA" rel="nofollow noopener">BooktTube Video: One &amp; Done </a>
  </u>
</b> - all about fabulous standalones! <br><br>Now that you know this one made the list - check out the video to see the rest!<br><br><b>
  <u>The Written Review</u>
</b>:<blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1567366972i/28085229._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><i>The question of what you want to own is actually the question of how you want to live your life.</i>
</blockquote>By no exaggeration, Marie Kond≈ç has spent <b>her entire life tidying.</b><br><br>She tidied when she was a child up until her adulthood - and over those decades, she's <b>learned a thing or two </b>about keeping a house clean.<blockquote>
  <i>The space in which we live should be for the person we are becoming now, not for the person we were in the past.</i>
</blockquote> <b>And the secret? <i>Joy.</i></b><br><br>That's right. The key to a clean house is joy - what makes us happy and how can we bring that more fully into our lives.<br><br>She's taken on clients from all over the world and has developed the <b>"Konmarie Method"</b> to tidying - starting with the impersonal (clothing) and proceeding into the memorable (keepsakes/heirlooms). <br><br>She's dealt with a wide variety of clients over the years, and has<b> developed instincts</b> for the best way to clear a house. And (more importantly) how to keep a house clean.<blockquote>
  <i>Clutter is caused by a failure to return things to where they belong. Therefore, storage should reduce the effort needed to put things away, not the effort needed to get them out.</i>
</blockquote>She wants to make the world<b> a happier, healthier and (above all) a tidier place.</b><br><br>Overall - I liked this one!<br><br>For as long as I could remember, my closet(s) practically overflowed with clothing (from years and years of keeping outfits "just-in-case").<blockquote>
  <i>But when we really delve into the reasons for why we can‚Äôt let something go, there are only two: an attachment to the past or a fear for the future.</i>
</blockquote>After reading her book/watching her Netflix series, I was able to cut out<b> all those extra clothes </b>and really make my wardrobe manageable! <br><br>One thing I found uniquely associated with her method is the way she<b> integrates joy into her tidying. </b>According to Marie, everything in your house should bring you joy - even the little things. <br><br>And while that seems impractical when first thinking of it (who ever thought "joy" when they look at a potato peeler?), the more I thought about it...the more I realized <b>she was right.</b><br><br>Taking the potato peeler example (which I saw a few GR reviewers throwing it around),<b> if you don't feel joy </b>holding it...try getting rid of it.<br><br>Next time you want to make mashed potatoes...think about how fun it is to use a knife to skin them. How many nicks you get on your hands. The loss of potato because of your awkward peeling.<br><br>A few rounds of that and gosh darn, you'll become grateful (even joyful) to have a potato peeler in your hands again.<br><br>And if you don't? Then I guess you never needed it in the first place and now your house has one less useless gadget.<br><br>Some of her <b>methods seem...questionable.</b>..especially to westerners. <br><br>For example, she recommends thanking each piece of clothing that you get rid of for a job well-done.<br><br>It seems silly, right? Utterly ridiculous. <br><br><b>And yet...<i>it works.</i></b><br><br>At the end of my clothing un-haul, I was left with several shirts that I honestly didn't want...but I also didn't want to give them away. (You know the type. They hang out in the back of your wardrobe for years...and yet no matter how little you wear them...they're always still there).<br><br>So, I tried the Konmarie method. I held each one in my hand - thinking, <i>Does this bring me joy?</i><br><br>If the answer was yes, I kept it. <br><br>If the answer was no, I thanked the article of clothing for being in my life and for giving me what I needed when I first bought it. And then I let it go.<br><br>And yes<b>, I felt silly doing it </b>- and I locked the bedroom door so that the hubby didn't know I was doing it - but at the same time, I felt closure. <br><br>I was accepting that the clothes had no part in my current life and that holding onto them any longer wasn't helping them or me.<b> It was time to set them free.</b><br><br>The only thing I disagreed upon was books. Marie believes (strongly) that books go in and out of lives and they aren't really to be kept.<blockquote>
  <i>For books, timing is everything. The moment you first encounter a particular book is the right time to read it. To avoid missing that moment, I recommend that you keep your collection small.</i>
</blockquote>Hmmm...does (literally) having a bookshelf in every room of the house (excluding the bathroom) count?<br><br> (And note: the only reason the bathroom doesn't have its own bookcase is because I worry about the damp)<br><br>My life is so intertwined with books that I honestly cannot see a time where my novels aren't the focal point of every room.<br><br>Perhaps rereading the book will give me some perspective...<br><br>All in all - <b>I loved learning her secrets to a tidy life. </b>My kitchen is wholly organized and I'm going to tackle the basement next. <br><br>There's something truly wonderful about stepping back from a long tidying session and just admiring how nice the house looks!<br><br><a href="http://bit.ly/33ii2CT" rel="nofollow noopener">YouTube</a> | <a href="http://www.mirandareads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/miranda.reads/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/Miranda_Reads" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | Snapchat @miranda.reads <br><br>Happy Reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9081042470650614423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1087709598" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1087709598" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1087709598?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=inspirational">inspirational</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=simplicity">simplicity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=how-to">how-to</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=domestic-bliss">domestic-bliss</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1087709598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15483588400438190175">This review was delayed because I was busy tidying up. <br/><br/>Yesterday I finished this charming little book about the Japanese art of decluttering and organizing, and this morning I woke up filled with <b>ENTHUSIASM</b> and was ready to open up every drawer and every closet and to simplify, simplify, simplify. <br/><br/>Of course, such projects always take longer than you expect. I was trying to follow the spirit of Marie Kondo's guidelines, which involve handling each one of your possessions and asking: Does this spa</span>
  <span id="freeText15483588400438190175" style="display:none">This review was delayed because I was busy tidying up. <br /><br />Yesterday I finished this charming little book about the Japanese art of decluttering and organizing, and this morning I woke up filled with <b>ENTHUSIASM</b> and was ready to open up every drawer and every closet and to simplify, simplify, simplify. <br /><br />Of course, such projects always take longer than you expect. I was trying to follow the spirit of Marie Kondo's guidelines, which involve handling each one of your possessions and asking: Does this spark joy? If it gives you happiness, keep it. Otherwise, get rid of it.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Imagine yourself living in a space that contains only things that spark joy. Isn't this the lifestyle you dream of? Keep only those things that speak to your heart. Then take the plunge and discard all the rest. By doing this you can reset your life and embark on a new lifestyle.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />After spending most of the day doing that, my donation pile had grown to several bags of clothes, a big bag of kitchen items, and a hefty stack of books. The most difficult things to sort were the books (a problem I bet most people reading this can relate to) and fortunately, Marie had some good advice on this:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />The most common reason for not discarding a book is "I might read it again." Take a moment to count the number of favorite books that you have actually read more than once ... In the end, you are going to read very few of your books again ... So when deciding which books to keep, forget about whether you think you'll read it again or whether you've mastered what's inside. Instead, take each book in your hand and decide whether it moves you or not. Keep only those books that will make you happy just to see them on your shelves, the ones that you really love.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />I have many, many books that make me happy, and I will keep those. But when I looked more closely at my shelves, I do have a fair number of books that I can pass on because they have served their purpose. <br /><br />Which brings me to one of my favorite aspects of Marie's advice, which is that when you get rid of something, you take a moment to appreciate the item and how it served you.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Each object has a different role to play. Not all clothes have come to you to be worn threadbare. It is the same with people. Not every person you meet in life will become a close friend or lover. Some you will find hard to get along with or impossible to like. But these people, too, teach you the precious lesson of who you <i>do</i> like, so that you will appreciate those special people even more. <br /><br />When you come across something that you cannot part with, think carefully about its true purpose in your life. You'll be surprised at how many of the things you possess have already fulfilled their role. By acknowledging their contribution and letting them go with gratitude, you will be able to truly put the things you own, and your life, in order. In the end, all that will remain are the things that you really treasure.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Marie is apparently a very successful and sought-after organizing consultant in Japan, and her book has numerous stories about her clients' efforts to declutter. Marie says many of her clients have a life-changing experience doing this. For example, after getting rid of all the books that didn't spark joy, one of Marie's clients realized that all of the books she kept were about social work, and that what she truly enjoyed in life was helping others, so she changed her career. Marie said this kind of epiphany often ocrrus because once we clear away the clutter in our lives, we can better see what truly matters to us.<br /><br />The anecdotes and guidelines were helpful, and this was a pleasant read, even though the Japanese-to-English translation was a bit stilted at times. I doubt I will take every piece of advice Marie gives ‚Äî- for example, she recommends putting all of your clothes on the floor, and then sorting them ‚Äî but I appreciated the spirit of it.<br /><br />Now I have to decide whether or not to keep this book: Does it spark joy?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15483588400438190175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who isn't the Silent Generation/Gen X/Gen Y</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1518528557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1192824667189810949">"<i>Be careful whose advice you buy but be patient with those who supply it. Advice is a form of nostalgia. Dispensing it is a way of fishing the past from the disposal, wiping it off, painting over the ugly parts and recycling it for more than it's worth.</i>" --Mary Schmich. <br/><br/>I'm trying, Mary. I really am. <br/><br/>Oh, screw it. <br/><br/>This was the most stressful book I've ever read. I haven't been thrown into such a fucking frenzy of hatred since <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28143721' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Thief</a>, and as with The Book Thief, I'm astounded that audienc</span>
  <span id="freeText1192824667189810949" style="display:none">"<i>Be careful whose advice you buy but be patient with those who supply it. Advice is a form of nostalgia. Dispensing it is a way of fishing the past from the disposal, wiping it off, painting over the ugly parts and recycling it for more than it's worth.</i>" --Mary Schmich. <br /><br />I'm trying, Mary. I really am. <br /><br />Oh, screw it. <br /><br />This was the most stressful book I've ever read. I haven't been thrown into such a fucking frenzy of hatred since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28143721" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Thief</a>, and as with The Book Thief, I'm astounded that audiences en masse are embracing such codswollop. <br /><br />I'm baffled as to why this is a bestseller. My best guess is that Marie Kondo targeted the most materialistic generation in the history of humanity, and they've since passed the book on to their equally superficial, spiritually empty, and stuff-obsessed grandchildren, who have made the fucking thing go viral. <br /><br />At this point, we should just accept the fact that when our fellow countrymen gobble up 4 million copies of a book, it's garbage. <br /><br />Seriously. Stupid just hit a whole new level.<br /><br />But before I go tearing the book and its semi-literate fans to pieces, let's be fair: I'm not the intended audience. Other than the fact that I'm an unduly harsh critic of everything I read (I like to call that using my brain, but whatever), I <i>already</i> live minimally: I live in one of the rainiest cities in the country, but I will never buy an umbrella; except for 4 absolute favorites, all of my books are in the Cloud; knick-knacks make make me want to smack someone, the mismatched mess of an "eclectic" decorating style nauseates me, and I never buy anything unless I need it or love it. My house is almost always immaculate, and I don't do clutter. Excess "stuff" stresses me out to no end. <br /><br />As I read Kondo's book, I realized that I'm not the typical American drowning in an excess of useless crap. (Living in Europe and trading continents 4 times in your 20s can do that to a person). So why wasn't I nodding in agreement with her guide to decluttering? <br /><br />You mean it's not obvious? Come on, people! <br /><br />Good God. When Americans' capacity for critical thinking has reached the level of blindly adopting all things Marie Kondo/KonMari, we've got bigger problems than "too much stuff." <br /><br />Look. There's no such thing as the "KonMari method for tidying up." Her ideas should only strike you as new if you've ignored the folding techniques of every retail store you've ever entered, or you've never poked through a Feng Shui catalog. Saying that you follow the "KonMari method for tidying up" is like saying you follow the "Harpo method for finding your spirit" or the "Martha Stewart Omnimedia method" of crafting Christmas ornaments out of pinecones and pipe cleaners.<br /><br />There <i> is</i> no KonMari method, you idiots. This isn't some ancient Japanese art of decluttering put forth by one diminutive woman from Tokyo. Marie Kondo was <a href="http://www.newyorker.com/books/page-turner/the-origin-story-of-marie-kondos-decluttering-empire" rel="nofollow noopener">manufactured by a Japanese publishing outlet</a>, and KonMari isn't a method, it's a <i>media company</i>. <br /><br />I'm not bothered by the woman-as-the-face-of-a-media-company thing. It's been done before. (Oprah and Martha Stewart, anyone?) What disgusts me about this book is the <b>deception</b> behind it. I don't dig Oprah, but at least she got people talking about uncomfortable topics like sexual assault and racism, among other things. And at least Martha Stewart was candid about her perfectionism and relentless focus on her business functioning as coping mechanisms during an ugly divorce. But Kondo? This chick is packaging her brand of crazy as the path to joy. <br /><br />I mean, peddling your mental illness as the new normal? Damn, that's cold. <br /><br />Look. If you're an American with an abundance of junk, you're normal. You're <i>fine</i>. Marie Kondo wants you to have a problem with your junk so she can make money. Dealing with her issues doesn't make her rich -- selling you her psychosis does.<br /><br />Do you really believe Kondo found joy in decluttering when she says her cleaning obsession started at age 5, and was a "custom [she] maintained even after entering high school," as she "sat on the floor for hours sorting things"? If you're going to ignore the fact that Kondo chose cleaning over normal after-school activities--a job, calling boys, playing sports--it's easy to brush aside her mention of having a teenage breakdown because her room wasn't clean enough. (Um, that's not a happy kid). Path to joy indeed. <br /><br />But we don't need to psychoanalyze the early years. Kondo admits that her passion for tidying "was motivated by a desire for recognition from [her] parents," and that she "had an unusually strong attachment to things" rather than people. (Hi, sad). But is a childless 20-something/former souvenir salesperson, fresh out of an unhappy childhood, really the one you want leading you down the supposed path to joy? Think about what this chick is saying:<br /><br /><i>"The purpose of a letter is fulfilled the moment it is received. By now, the person who wrote it has long forgotten what he or she wrote and even the letter's very existence."</i> <br />Jesus. That's a bleak outlook on life. <br />But I guess Kondo is right. My grandma doesn't give a shit about the letters she wrote me--she's dead. Then again, I don't hold on to letters from grandma for her sake. <br /><br /><i>"Aim for perfection."</i> <br />Jesus CHRIST. The only thing I hate more than knick-knacks and the eclectic is a living space created with "perfection" in mind. "Perfect" living spaces are stressful. They're goddamned mausoleums void of character and humanity. There's a little genius in a (small) organized mess. A tad bit of clutter is humanizing. There can be beauty in a bit of chaos. <br />Hey, Marie, here's an idea: get outside more. Perfection is a fleeting organic moment: a newborn baby, a sunset, the Fibonacci sequence in the florets of a flower. It's not some state you declutter your way into. <br /><br /><i>"Move all of your storage units into your closet. This is where I usually put steel racks, bookcases, and cupboards or shelves, which can also be used to store books."</i> <br />This. Right here. <br />This is exactly why I found this book so goddamned irritating. Passages like this made my immaculate and clutter-free city apartment feel like it wasn't good enough. <br />Take my bookcase. I <i>hate</i> bookcases. I view them as a way of storing junk, and in my 30-something years, I've only seen one bookcase done well. But I have a bookcase for my 6 year-old. (No goddamn way am I going to put his books on the Cloud, giving him another excuse to stare at a screen). <br />I was never bothered by the bookcase until I read Kondo's book, but now I <i>can't wait</i> until we can throw the damn thing away. And moving it out of sight will magically make me hate it less?<br />Yeah, no. This is my son's house, too. <br />Sorry, Marie, I'm not going let your book make me miserable about a kid's bookcase. I'll go back to not noticing it. Thanks. <br /><br /><i>Never, ever tie up your stockings. Never, ever ball up your socks. </i><br />God! Who the fuck cares about how they fold their socks? I'd love to scribble all over Kondo's walls just to see what she'd do. <br /><br /><i>"Transform your closet into your own private space, one that gives you the thrill of pleasure.</i><br />Heh. An organized closet sparking a "thrill of pleasure"? I'd recommend another human being or a battery-powered...never mind, get your "thrill of pleasure" wherever, it's not my business. <br /><br /><i>"When you stand in front of a closet that has been reorganized...your heart will beat faster and the cells in your body buzz with energy."</i> <br />Isn't it weird that Kondo describes an organized closet with words generally associated with falling in love/physical intimacy? Well, that's...fucked up, but whatever. <br />I had an altogether different experience. <br />When I upgraded to a new apartment a few months ago, I organized my hall closet. Afterwards, I stood there wondering if I'd accomplished anything or just wasted a bunch of time. When my 6 year-old wandered up and, near tears said, "When you clean, we don't get to play," I went ahead and decided on the latter. <br /><br /><i>This is the routine I follow every day when I return home from work. First, I unlock the door and announce to my house, 'I'm home!' Picking up the pair of shoes I wore yesterday...I say, 'Thank you very much for you hard work,' and put them away...I put my jacket and dress on a hanger, say 'Good job!'...I put [my handbag] on the top shelf of the closet, saying 'You did well. Have a good rest.'" </i> <br />Um. She's <b>talking to her stuff</b>. What the f%$#?!?!<br />And why are Americans so quick to dismiss Kondo's talking to inanimate objects as some cultural quirk? No one talks to their shit in Japan unless they're certifiably nuts. <br /><br /><i>"The best way to choose what to keep and what to throw away is to</i> <b>take each item in one's hand and ask: 'Does this spark joy?'</b>" <br />LOL, the "wisdom" of people under 30. <br />Anyone <a href="http://www.thestar.com/life/relationships/2016/01/14/why-decluttering-guru-marie-kondo-is-wrong.html" rel="nofollow noopener">who has kids (or a general understanding of life)</a> knows this is fucking ridiculous.<br />I mean, give me a break! Going all slash and burn on your life, save for items that "spark joy?" I wonder what people who've lost everything in a fire would say about that? I'm sure people who survived major disasters would *totally* enlighten you about the *joy* sparked from their <i>stuff</i>. <br />Obviously, if your mountain of junk makes you miserable, your stuff owns you. But if you Kondo-ize your house until you only have things that "bring you joy," your reduced pile of stuff <i>still</i> owns you.<br />Face it. If you're looking for joy in the material, you don't need Marie Kondo--you need to reevaluate your life. <br /><br />Okay, fine. Maybe I'm being unfair. <br /><br />People are indeed affected by their environment, and decluttering can feel satisfying, even cleansing. But look who's telling you how to go about it: a chick whose childhood obsession with cleaning came from trying to please others, whose sole work experience includes selling junk at shrines, and whose descriptions of "joy" include <b>rules, repetition, ritual,</b> and <b>talking to inanimate objects.</b> <br /><br />Yeah. They make medication for that. <br /><br />At this point, I should pick up Marie Kondo's book and ask myself whether it sparks joy. Well, no, it actually sparks rage. To the trash with it, then! <br /><br />SUCKED.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1192824667189810949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText965486152172805056" style="display:none">I so agree that it is life-changing magic when everything has been tidied up. But I don't need to read the book as I already have this magic. Her name is Cynthia and she comes every other Thursday morning. <br /><br />Admittedly she costs a "bit" more than this book and wants a computer lesson during her time but she Fabuloso's my floors, the airing cupboard is stacked with neatly-folded linens and once in a while we go at a place (like my son's room) that lacks 'magic' and garbage-bag everything. This is a boring job alone and I can't bear to throw things away. But Cynthia can. She has no emotional attachment to anything and couldn't care less if my son might object to having something or other thrown away. Not that he ever remembers when he comes back from college.<br /><br />I bet you with all the money the sales of this book engenders that the author will no longer be cleaning and tidying up her house herself but also get a magic Cynthia of her own.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1090000103" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1090000103" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/666282-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556719821p2/666282.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1090000103?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/666282-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1090000103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1598084570221077736">Because I have such admiration for the Japanese aesthetic, I picked up this book with interest. In fact, I was delighted to discover that my lifetime habits of tidying are roughly in line with Marie Kondo's, the expert's, except for a brilliant bit of advice relative to clothes that I can't wait to try. Kondo's basic mantra is "keep things that bring you joy; discard everything else," and that is so cool that I'm going to try it, because it goes further than what I had thought, but along the sam</span>
  <span id="freeText1598084570221077736" style="display:none">Because I have such admiration for the Japanese aesthetic, I picked up this book with interest. In fact, I was delighted to discover that my lifetime habits of tidying are roughly in line with Marie Kondo's, the expert's, except for a brilliant bit of advice relative to clothes that I can't wait to try. Kondo's basic mantra is "keep things that bring you joy; discard everything else," and that is so cool that I'm going to try it, because it goes further than what I had thought, but along the same lines. My goal is to have a house as clear and clutter-free as a Japanese tea-ceremony room or house. I've got a ways to go, but lots of joy to discover. If you're a pack rat, read this book now, and get started. If you're already on the tidy brigade, this book will help you up your game. <br /><br />Additional thoughts: there is a third category, beyond "things that bring you joy," and "things that don't," and that is "things that you gotta have but can't really be expected to bring you joy." I have a couple of garden hoses, hung on a bracket in my garage. They don't bring me joy; they bring me chores, occasionally. But I would be foolish to get rid of them. I wish Kondo had addressed this third category. Much as I delighted in this book, and appreciate its goals, it seems utopian now after wrestling with the ideas in it for a few weeks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1598084570221077736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1328966228" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jenna " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24806763-jenna"><img alt="Jenna " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1396102867p2/24806763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1328966228?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna " href="/user/show/24806763-jenna">Jenna </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24806763-jenna?shelf=can-i-have-those-hours-back">can-i-have-those-hours-back</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24806763-jenna?shelf=lifestyle">lifestyle</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328966228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3573601695800356088">I give this one 2 stars because I took a few things from it that I think are great ideas to help me organize. But about a third of the book was spent repeating itself and when it wasn't doing that...it was just weird to me. I wondered at times if the book was satire or a memoir of OCD disguised as the middle child syndrome. Some examples that had me wondering if this was satire or not include:<br/><br/><blockquote>1) She talked as though inanimate objects have feelings or as though they are alive so you have to say g</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText3573601695800356088" style="display:none">I give this one 2 stars because I took a few things from it that I think are great ideas to help me organize. But about a third of the book was spent repeating itself and when it wasn't doing that...it was just weird to me. I wondered at times if the book was satire or a memoir of OCD disguised as the middle child syndrome. Some examples that had me wondering if this was satire or not include:<br /><br /><blockquote>1) She talked as though inanimate objects have feelings or as though they are alive so you have to say goodbye to them and a nice thought before throwing them away. You don't want to hurt their feelings since they no longer bring you joy. She thinks that socks shouldn't be balled up because they deserve to rest comfortably since they are a medium being rubbed between your feet and your shoes. Poor socks.<br /><br />2) When she goes to someone's house to help "tidy" (organize) she bows in the middle of the room and greets it and also thanks it for allowing her to tidy it up. Basically she and the room connect at an intimate level before she goes stripping it down to its bareness. Oh, and she wears a dress and blazer when tidying to show respect to the house (not its occupant) and thinks you should too. Sweat pants are a big no-no ladies! And don't forget to clean out your purses on a daily basis. No bloated purses for you.<br /><br />3) She claims that tidying rids the body of toxins and that she sees it quite often. She claims that it clears the skin and trims the waistline. So don't be alarmed if after purging you get a pimple or your belly starts to rumble. She said that after one of her clients tidied up her space she immediately had diarrhea (yes, she went there) as though her body was purging its junk out too.<br /><br />4) Once when she replaced her cell phone, she was sure to send her previous phone a text message thanking it for its service. When she did this, her phone (that always worked properly) died and never worked again. It was like it knew it's job was done.</blockquote><br /><br />I would have probably taken more from this book if there was less of the above type of examples. Now I just think the book is a bit kooky.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3573601695800356088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Lianne Downey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18079825-lianne-downey"><img alt="Lianne Downey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442517583p2/18079825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1121509454?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lianne Downey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lianne Downey" href="/user/show/18079825-lianne-downey">Lianne Downey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18079825-lianne-downey?shelf=very-special">very-special</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1121509454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11911686468438813919">I have just experienced the life-changing magic of discarding two-thirds of my clothes, shoes, and accessories! One category down, a whole house to go. It's so fun to get dressed now; all I have to do is reach in and anything I pull out "sparks joy" in my heart. After going up three sizes and then back down again within the last 2 years, you'd think I wouldn't have anything left to discard, but that wasn't true. So many items were hiding the good stuff, dragging me down with bad memories, worn o</span>
  <span id="freeText11911686468438813919" style="display:none">I have just experienced the life-changing magic of discarding two-thirds of my clothes, shoes, and accessories! One category down, a whole house to go. It's so fun to get dressed now; all I have to do is reach in and anything I pull out "sparks joy" in my heart. After going up three sizes and then back down again within the last 2 years, you'd think I wouldn't have anything left to discard, but that wasn't true. So many items were hiding the good stuff, dragging me down with bad memories, worn out associations, and/or poor lines and fit.<br /><br />After reading a few chapters, my husband has already begun with his own clothes, and miracle of miracles, he immediately bought some new work pants <i>in his own style, of his own choosing</i>, the kind that "spark joy" for him. And this man hates to shop. <br /><br />So, life-changing? Yes! Does the book coincide with everything I've learned in the last 40 years of studying the energetic nature of human beings and all life as we know it? Yes! Do I love this book? Yes! <br /><br />Am I the kind of person who, even though I recently undertook another de-cluttering project, still lived amid stashes of things that weighed me down with guilt and obligation and insecurity? Yes! My husband and I experienced some scary poverty years early in our lives together. It's been difficult ever since to resist the urge to keep more than we need. As the youngest in our respective families, we also shared the habit of accepting whatever was given to us, hand-me-downs that might or might not have suited us. The concept of wearing, using, or keeping only items that "spark joy" makes brilliant sense to me now, and feels like wonderful liberation.<br /><br />I am very excited and inspired by Marie Kondo's work, and I'm equally enthusiastic about continuing throughout the house until we are living a much smaller, less encumbered, joyously infused material existence here in our happy home. I love the way she characterizes the impact of our thoughts on our things, and vice versa. I just love her entire outlook. <br /><br />Oh yes, one more life-changing aspect (already): I hated to fold things. Just like she says, after following her instructions (and watching her demonstrate on YouTube) it's become a fun game for me. Weird, but true. You have to experience this to believe it. I recommend reading the entire book before you begin any "tidying-up," though, so you get the full effect of Marie Kondo‚Äôs charismatic, inspirational advice.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11911686468438813919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1125430601" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1125430601" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6422426-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362506030p2/6422426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1125430601?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mia" href="/user/show/6422426-mia">Mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6422426-mia?shelf=japanese">japanese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6422426-mia?shelf=psych-and-self-help">psych-and-self-help</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6422426-mia?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1125430601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3768753572612318964">I hesitated to read this book because I thought it might not have much to offer beyond what's written on its back cover: get rid of any belongings that don't "spark joy." That is really the essence of Kondo's advice, but she has more to offer in the rest of the book too, and it's surprisingly eccentric and Japanese. No American book would advise praying to your house before tidying it, for example, or suggest that objects just want to help you and that, once released from your possession, they w</span>
  <span id="freeText3768753572612318964" style="display:none">I hesitated to read this book because I thought it might not have much to offer beyond what's written on its back cover: get rid of any belongings that don't "spark joy." That is really the essence of Kondo's advice, but she has more to offer in the rest of the book too, and it's surprisingly eccentric and Japanese. No American book would advise praying to your house before tidying it, for example, or suggest that objects just want to help you and that, once released from your possession, they will continue reflecting their helpful energy back to you from wherever they are.<br /><br />It seems that these suggestions make Kondo unusual even in Japan, but her Shinto-influenced outlook is so much like what my mother raised me with that it was exactly what I needed to work through the guilt that keeps me clinging to objects I don't use or even particularly like. Because she spoke my language, I decided to take her suggestions literally, even the odd ones. So I lit some incense, clapped a couple of times, and prayed to my apartment. A breeze blew in through my crappy vertical blinds, and I could imagine my apartment feeling glad for the recognition.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3768753572612318964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
